<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Classical Greek Athenian philosopher (c. 470 – 399 BC)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the classical Greek philosopher. For other uses of Socrates, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Socrates_(disambiguation)" title="Socrates (disambiguation)">Socrates (disambiguation)</a>. For the Attic orator, see <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a>.</div>

<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Virtually all subsequent <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a>, especially his followers, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antisthenes" title="Antisthenes">Antisthenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristippus" title="Aristippus">Aristippus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaedo_of_Elis" title="Phaedo of Elis">Phaedo of Elis</a></div></li></ul>

<p><b>Socrates</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2006_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2006-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A3%CF%89%CE%BA%CF%81%CE%AC%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82" title="wikt:Σωκράτης">Σωκράτης</a></span></span>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 470</span>–399 BC) was a <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> philosopher from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a> who is credited as the founder of <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> and among the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_philosopher" title="Moral philosopher">moral philosophers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> tradition of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> An enigmatic figure, Socrates authored no texts and is known mainly through the posthumous accounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_writers" title="Classical writers">classical writers</a>, particularly his students <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>. These accounts are written as <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogues</a>, in which Socrates and his interlocutors examine a subject in the style of question and answer; they gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Socratic_dialogue" title="Socratic dialogue">Socratic dialogue</a> literary genre. Contradictory accounts of Socrates make a reconstruction of his philosophy nearly impossible, a situation known as the <a href="/wiki/Socratic_problem" title="Socratic problem">Socratic problem</a>. Socrates was a polarizing figure in Athenian society. In 399 BC, he was accused of <a href="/wiki/Asebeia" title="Asebeia">impiety</a> and corrupting the youth. After <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Socrates" title="Trial of Socrates">a trial</a> that lasted a day, he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sentenced_to_death" title="Sentenced to death">sentenced to death</a>. He spent his last day in prison, refusing offers to help him escape.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato%27s_dialogues" title="Plato's dialogues">Plato's dialogues</a> are among the most comprehensive accounts of Socrates to survive from antiquity. They demonstrate the Socratic approach to areas of philosophy including <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>. The Platonic Socrates lends his name to the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">Socratic method</a>, and also to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socratic_irony" title="Socratic irony">Socratic irony</a>. The Socratic method of questioning, or <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method#Method" title="Socratic method">elenchus</a>, takes shape in dialogue using short questions and answers, epitomized by those Platonic texts in which Socrates and his interlocutors examine various aspects of an issue or an abstract meaning, usually relating to one of the virtues, and find themselves at an <a href="/wiki/Aporia" title="Aporia">impasse</a>, completely unable to define what they thought they understood. Socrates is known for <a href="/wiki/I_know_that_I_know_nothing" title="I know that I know nothing">proclaiming his total ignorance</a>; he used to say that the only thing he was aware of was his ignorance, seeking to imply that the realization of our ignorance is the first step in philosophizing.
</p><p>Socrates exerted a strong influence on philosophers in later <a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">antiquity</a> and has continued to do so in the <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern era</a>. Socrates was studied by medieval and Islamic scholars and played an important role in the thought of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, particularly within the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanist movement</a>. Interest in Socrates continued unabated, as reflected in the works of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>. Depictions of Socrates in art, literature, and popular culture have made him a widely known figure in the Western philosophical tradition.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-4"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_and_the_Socratic_problem">Sources and the Socratic problem</span></h2>

<p>Socrates did not document his teachings. All that is known about him comes from the accounts of others: mainly the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and the historian <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>, who were both his pupils; the Athenian comic dramatist <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a> (Socrates's contemporary); and Plato's pupil <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, who was born after Socrates's death. The often contradictory stories from these ancient accounts only serve to complicate scholars' ability to reconstruct Socrates's true thoughts reliably, a predicament known as the <a href="/wiki/Socratic_problem" title="Socratic problem">Socratic problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie19725–7Dorion20111–2May20009Waterfield20131_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie19725–7Dorion20111–2May20009Waterfield20131-3">[3]</a></sup> The works of Plato, Xenophon, and other authors who use the character of Socrates as an investigative tool, are written in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogue</a> between Socrates and his interlocutors and provide the main source of information on Socrates's life and thought. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socratic_dialogues" title="Socratic dialogues">Socratic dialogues</a> (<i>logos sokratikos</i>) was a term coined by Aristotle to describe this newly formed literary genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200020Dorion20117Waterfield20131_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200020Dorion20117Waterfield20131-4">[4]</a></sup> While the exact dates of their composition are unknown, some were probably written after Socrates's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDöring201124–25_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDöring201124–25-5">[5]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> first noted, the extent to which the dialogues portray Socrates authentically is a matter of some debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20117–9_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20117–9-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plato_and_Xenophon">Plato and Xenophon</span></h3>
<p>An honest man, Xenophon was no trained philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197213–15_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197213–15-7">[7]</a></sup> He could neither fully conceptualize nor articulate Socrates's arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215-8">[8]</a></sup> He admired Socrates for his intelligence, patriotism, and courage on the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215-8">[8]</a></sup> He discusses Socrates in four works: the <i><a href="/wiki/Memorabilia_(Xenophon)" title="Memorabilia (Xenophon)">Memorabilia</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Oeconomicus" title="Oeconomicus">Oeconomicus</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Xenophon)" title="Symposium (Xenophon)">Symposium</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Apology_(Xenophon)" title="Apology (Xenophon)">Apology of Socrates</a></i>. He also mentions a story featuring Socrates in his <i>Anabasis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215–16_&amp;_28_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215–16_&amp;_28-9">[9]</a></sup> <i>Oeconomicus</i> recounts a discussion on practical agricultural issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215–16_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215–16-10">[10]</a></sup> Like Plato's <a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)"><i>Apology</i></a>, Xenophon's <i>Apologia</i> describes the trial of Socrates, but the works diverge substantially and, according to <a href="/wiki/W._K._C._Guthrie" title="W. K. C. Guthrie">W. K. C. Guthrie</a>, Xenophon's account portrays a Socrates of "intolerable smugness and complacency".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197218_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197218-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>Symposium</i> is a dialogue of Socrates with other prominent Athenians during an after-dinner discussion, but is quite different from Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i>: there is no overlap in the guest list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197220–23_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197220–23-12">[12]</a></sup> In <i>Memorabilia</i>, he defends Socrates from the accusations of corrupting the youth and being against the gods; essentially, it is a collection of various stories gathered together to construct a new <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apologia" title="wikt:apologia">apology</a> for Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197225–26_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197225–26-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato's representation of Socrates is not straightforward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197229–31Dorion20116_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197229–31Dorion20116-14">[14]</a></sup> Plato was a pupil of Socrates and outlived him by five decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197230_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197230-15">[15]</a></sup> How trustworthy Plato is in representing the attributes of Socrates is a matter of debate; the view that he did not represent views other than Socrates's own is not shared by many contemporary scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197229–33Waterfield20133–4_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197229–33Waterfield20133–4-16">[16]</a></sup> A driver of this doubt is the inconsistency of the character of Socrates that he presents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200020Dorion20116–7_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200020Dorion20116–7-17">[17]</a></sup> One common explanation of this inconsistency is that Plato initially tried to accurately represent the historical Socrates, while later in his writings he was happy to insert his own views into Socrates's words. Under this understanding, there is a distinction between the <i>Socratic</i> Socrates of Plato's earlier works and the <i>Platonic</i> Socrates of Plato's later writings, although the boundary between the two seems blurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200020Waterfield20133–4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200020Waterfield20133–4-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenophon's and Plato's accounts differ in their presentations of Socrates as a person. Xenophon's Socrates is duller, less humorous and less ironic than Plato's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197215-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200019–20_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200019–20-19">[19]</a></sup> Xenophon's Socrates also lacks the philosophical features of Plato's Socrates—ignorance, the Socratic method or <i>elenchus</i>—and thinks <i><a href="/wiki/Enkrateia" title="Enkrateia">enkrateia</a></i> (self-control) is of pivotal importance, which is not the case with Plato's Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20114,_10_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20114,_10-20">[20]</a></sup> Generally, <i>logoi Sokratikoi</i> cannot help us to reconstruct the historical Socrates even in cases where their narratives overlap, as authors may have influenced each other's accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterfield201310–13_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterfield201310–13-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristophanes_and_other_sources">Aristophanes and other sources</span></h3>
<p>Writers of Athenian comedy, including Aristophanes, also commented on Socrates. Aristophanes's most important comedy with respect to Socrates is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Clouds" title="The Clouds">The Clouds</a></i>, in which Socrates is a central character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197239–41_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197239–41-22">[22]</a></sup> In this drama, Aristophanes presents a caricature of Socrates that leans towards sophism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197239–51_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197239–51-23">[23]</a></sup> ridiculing Socrates as an absurd atheist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe20115_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe20115-24">[24]</a></sup> Socrates in <i>Clouds</i> is interested in natural philosophy, which conforms to Plato's depiction of him in <i>Phaedo</i>. What is certain is that by the age of 45, Socrates had already captured the interest of Athenians as a philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKonstan201185,_88_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKonstan201185,_88-25">[25]</a></sup> It is not clear whether Aristophanes's work is useful in reconstructing the historical Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterfield20137–8_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterfield20137–8-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other ancient authors who wrote about Socrates were <a href="/wiki/Aeschines_of_Sphettus" title="Aeschines of Sphettus">Aeschines of Sphettus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antisthenes" title="Antisthenes">Antisthenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristippus" title="Aristippus">Aristippus</a>, Bryson, Cebes, <a href="/wiki/Crito" title="Crito">Crito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>, Phaedo and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, all of whom wrote after Socrates's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVlastos199152Kahn19981–2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVlastos199152Kahn19981–2-27">[27]</a></sup> Aristotle was not a contemporary of Socrates; he studied under Plato at the latter's Academy for twenty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197235–36Waterfield20138_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197235–36Waterfield20138-28">[28]</a></sup> Aristotle treats Socrates without the bias of Xenophon and Plato, who had an emotional tie with Socrates, and he scrutinizes Socrates's doctrines as a philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197238_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197238-29">[29]</a></sup> Aristotle was familiar with the various written and unwritten stories of Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197238–39_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197238–39-30">[30]</a></sup> His role in understanding Socrates is limited. He does not write extensively on Socrates; and, when he does, he is mainly preoccupied with the early dialogues of Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion201111,_16Waterfield201311_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion201111,_16Waterfield201311-31">[31]</a></sup> There are also general doubts on his reliability on the history of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterfield20138–11_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterfield20138–11-32">[32]</a></sup> Still, his testimony is vital in understanding Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterfield201311_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterfield201311-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Socratic_problem">The Socratic problem</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Socratic_problem" title="Socratic problem">Socratic problem</a></div>
<p>In a seminal work titled "The Worth of Socrates as a Philosopher" (1818), the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schleiermacher" title="Friedrich Schleiermacher">Friedrich Schleiermacher</a> attacked Xenophon's accounts; his attack was widely accepted and gave rise to the Socratic problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20111–3_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20111–3-34">[34]</a></sup> Schleiermacher criticized Xenophon for his naïve representation of Socrates. Xenophon was a soldier, argued Schleiermacher, and was therefore not well placed to articulate Socratic ideas. Furthermore, Xenophon was biased in his depiction of his former friend and teacher: he believed Socrates was treated unfairly by Athens, and sought to prove his point of view rather than to provide an impartial account. The result, said Schleiermacher, was that Xenophon portrayed Socrates as an uninspiring philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20112–3_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20112–3-35">[35]</a></sup> By the early 20th century, Xenophon's account was largely rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20115_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20115-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Karl_Joel_(philosopher)" title="Karl Joel (philosopher)">Karl Joel</a>, basing his arguments on Aristotle's interpretation of <i>logos sokratikos</i>, suggested that the Socratic dialogues are mostly fictional: according to Joel, the dialogues' authors were just mimicking some Socratic traits of dialogue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion20117–10_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion20117–10-37">[37]</a></sup> In the mid-20th century, philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Olof_Gigon" title="Olof Gigon">Olof Gigon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Dupr%C3%A9el" title="Eugène Dupréel">Eugène Dupréel</a>, based on Joel's arguments, proposed that the study of Socrates should focus on the various versions of his character and beliefs rather than aiming to reconstruct a historical Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion201112–14_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion201112–14-38">[38]</a></sup> Later, ancient philosophy scholar <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Vlastos" title="Gregory Vlastos">Gregory Vlastos</a> suggested that the early Socratic dialogues of Plato were more compatible with other evidence for a historical Socrates than his later writings, an argument that is based on inconsistencies in Plato's own evolving depiction of Socrates. Vlastos totally disregarded Xenophon's account except when it agreed with Plato's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion201112–14_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion201112–14-38">[38]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Kahn" title="Charles H. Kahn">Charles H. Kahn</a> has reinforced the skeptical stance on the unsolvable Socratic problem, suggesting that only Plato's <i>Apology</i> has any historical significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorion201117–18_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorion201117–18-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_(detail).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1570" data-file-width="2144" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Potidaea_Socrates_saving_Alcibiades_(detail).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Potidaea" title="Battle of Potidaea">Battle of Potidaea</a> (432 BC): Athenians against Corinthians (detail). Scene of Socrates (center) saving <a href="/wiki/Alcibiades" title="Alcibiades">Alcibiades</a>. 18th century engraving. According to Plato, Socrates participated in the Battle of Potidaea, the retreat of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delium" title="Battle of Delium">Battle of Delium</a> and the battle of Amphipolis (422 BC)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie19722_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie19722-40">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Socrates was born in 470 or 469 BC to <a href="/wiki/Sophroniscus" title="Sophroniscus">Sophroniscus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phaenarete" title="Phaenarete">Phaenarete</a>, a stoneworker and a midwife, respectively, in the Athenian <a href="/wiki/Deme" title="Deme">deme</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alopece" title="Alopece">Alopece</a>; therefore, he was an Athenian citizen, having been born to relatively affluent Athenians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOber2010159–160Ahbel-Rappe20111Guthrie197258Dorion201112Nails2020A_chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_in_the_context_of_Athenian_history_and_the_dramatic_dates_of_Plato's_dialogues_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOber2010159–160Ahbel-Rappe20111Guthrie197258Dorion201112Nails2020A_chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_in_the_context_of_Athenian_history_and_the_dramatic_dates_of_Plato's_dialogues-41">[41]</a></sup> He lived close to his father's relatives and inherited, as was customary, part of his father's estate, securing a life reasonably free of financial concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOber2010160–161_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOber2010160–161-42">[42]</a></sup> His education followed the laws and customs of Athens. He learned the basic skills of reading and writing and, like most wealthy Athenians, received extra lessons in various other fields such as gymnastics, poetry and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOber2010161–162_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOber2010161–162-43">[43]</a></sup> He was married twice (which came first is not clear): his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Xanthippe" title="Xanthippe">Xanthippe</a> took place when Socrates was in his fifties, and another marriage was with a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Aristides" title="Aristides">Aristides</a>, an Athenian statesman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOber2010161_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOber2010161-44">[44]</a></sup> He had three sons with Xanthippe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265-45">[45]</a></sup> Socrates fulfilled his military service during the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> and distinguished himself in three campaigns, according to Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197259_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197259-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another incident that reflects Socrates's respect for the law is the arrest of <a href="/wiki/Leon_of_Salamis" title="Leon of Salamis">Leon the Salaminian</a>. As Plato describes in his <i><a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)">Apology</a></i>, Socrates and four others were summoned to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tholos_(Athens)" title="Tholos (Athens)">Tholos</a> and told by representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Tyrants" title="Thirty Tyrants">Thirty Tyrants</a> (which began ruling in 404 BC) to arrest Leon for execution. Again Socrates was the sole abstainer, choosing to risk the tyrants' wrath and retribution rather than to participate in what he considered to be a crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265Ober2010167–171_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265Ober2010167–171-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates attracted great interest from the Athenian public and especially the Athenian youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197278_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197278-48">[48]</a></sup> He was notoriously ugly, having a flat turned-up nose, bulging eyes and a large belly; his friends joked about his appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197266–67_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197266–67-49">[49]</a></sup> Socrates was indifferent to material pleasures, including his own appearance and personal comfort. He neglected personal hygiene, bathed rarely, walked <a href="/wiki/Barefoot" title="Barefoot">barefoot</a>, and owned only one ragged coat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197269_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197269-50">[50]</a></sup> He moderated his eating, drinking, and sex, although he did not practice full abstention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197269_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197269-50">[50]</a></sup> Although Socrates was attracted to youth, as was common and accepted in ancient Greece, he resisted his passion for young men because, as Plato describes, he was more interested in educating their souls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197270–75Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197270–75Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness-51">[51]</a></sup> Socrates did not seek sex from his disciples, as was often the case between older and younger men in Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013210–211Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013210–211Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness-52">[52]</a></sup> Politically, he did not take sides in the rivalry between the democrats and the oligarchs in Athens; he criticized both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197292–94Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197292–94Nails2020Socrates's_strangeness-53">[53]</a></sup> The character of Socrates as exhibited in <i><a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)">Apology</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crito" title="Crito">Crito</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaedo</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i> concurs with other sources to an extent that gives confidence in Plato's depiction of Socrates in these works as being representative of the real Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKahn199875_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKahn199875-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates died in Athens in 399 BC after a trial for <a href="/wiki/Asebeia" title="Asebeia">impiety</a> and the corruption of the young that lasted for only a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201115–19_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201115–19-55">[55]</a></sup> He spent his last day in prison among friends and followers who offered him a route to escape, which he refused. He died the next morning, in accordance with his sentence, after drinking <a href="/wiki/Conium_maculatum" title="Conium maculatum">poison hemlock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201117,_21_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201117,_21-56">[56]</a></sup> He had never left Athens, except during the military campaigns which he had participated in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201110_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201110-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial_of_Socrates">Trial of Socrates</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Socrates" title="Trial of Socrates">Trial of Socrates</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_unexamined_life_is_not_worth_living" title="The unexamined life is not worth living">The unexamined life is not worth living</a></div>
<p>In 399 BC, Socrates went on trial for corrupting the minds of the youth of Athens, and for impiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200030_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200030-58">[58]</a></sup> Socrates defended himself unsuccessfully. He was found guilty by a majority vote cast by a jury of hundreds of male Athenian citizens and, according to the custom, proposed his own penalty: that <i>he</i> should be given free food and housing by the state, for the services he rendered to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48-59">[59]</a></sup> In the alternative, he proposed that he be fined one <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mina" title="wikt:mina">mina</a> of silver (according to him, all he had).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48-59">[59]</a></sup> The jurors declined his offer and ordered the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48-59">[59]</a></sup> The official charges were: (1) corrupting youth; (2) worshipping false gods; and (3) not worshipping the state religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200040_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200040-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates was charged in a politically tense climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup> In 404 BC, the Athenians had been crushed by Spartans at the decisive naval <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aegospotami" title="Battle of Aegospotami">Battle of Aegospotami</a>, and subsequently, the Spartans laid siege to Athens. They replaced the democratic government with a new, pro-oligarchic government, named the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Tyrants" title="Thirty Tyrants">Thirty Tyrants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup> Because of their tyrannical measures, some Athenians organized to overthrow the Tyrants—and, indeed, they managed to do so briefly—until a Spartan request for aid from the Thirty arrived and a compromise was sought. When the Spartans left again, however, democrats seized the opportunity to kill the oligarchs and reclaim the government of Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The accusations against Socrates were initiated by a poet, <a href="/wiki/Meletus" title="Meletus">Meletus</a>, who asked for the death penalty in accordance with the charge of <i><a href="/wiki/Asebeia" title="Asebeia">asebeia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup> Other accusers were <a href="/wiki/Anytus" title="Anytus">Anytus</a> and Lycon. After a month or two, in late spring or early summer, the trial started and likely went on for most of one day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup> There were two main sources for the religion-based accusations. First, Socrates had rejected the anthropomorphism of traditional Greek religion by denying that the gods did bad things like humans do. Second, he seemed to believe in a <i>daimonion</i>—an inner voice with, as his accusers suggested, divine origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2020A_Chronology_of_the_historical_Socrates-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato's <i>Apology</i> starts with Socrates answering the various rumours against him that have given rise to the indictment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200031_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200031-62">[62]</a></sup> First, Socrates defends himself against the rumour that he is an atheist <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">naturalist philosopher</a>, as portrayed in Aristophanes's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Clouds" title="The Clouds">The Clouds</a></i>; or a <a href="/wiki/Sophist" title="Sophist">sophist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200033–39_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200033–39-63">[63]</a></sup> Against the allegations of corrupting the youth, Socrates answers that he has never corrupted anyone intentionally, since corrupting someone would carry the risk of being corrupted back in return, and that would be illogical, since corruption is undesirable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200041–42_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200041–42-64">[64]</a></sup> On the second charge, Socrates asks for clarification. Meletus responds by repeating the accusation that Socrates is an atheist. Socrates notes the contradiction between atheism and worshipping false gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200042_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200042-65">[65]</a></sup> He then claims that he is "God's gift" to the Athenians, since his activities ultimately benefit Athens; thus, in condemning him to death, Athens itself will be the greatest loser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200043_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200043-66">[66]</a></sup> After that, he says that even though no human can reach wisdom, seeking it is the best thing someone can do, implying money and prestige are not as precious as commonly thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200045–46_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200045–46-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2559" data-file-width="3896" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/220px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/330px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/440px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Socrates" title="The Death of Socrates">The Death of Socrates</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a> (1787). Socrates was visited by friends in his last night at prison. His discussion with them gave rise to Plato's <i>Crito</i> and <i>Phaedo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265–66_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197265–66-68">[68]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Socrates was given the chance to offer alternative punishments for himself after being found guilty. He could have requested permission to flee Athens and live in exile, but he did not do so. Instead, according to Plato, he requested that a fine should be imposed on him and also suggested that free meals should be provided for him daily in recognition of his worth to Athens. According to Xenophon, however, Socrates made no proposals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197263–65Ahbel-Rappe2011Ober2010146_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197263–65Ahbel-Rappe2011Ober2010146-69">[69]</a></sup> The jurors favoured the death penalty by making him drink a cup of <a href="/wiki/Conium_maculatum" title="Conium maculatum">hemlock</a> (a poisonous liquid).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197264–65_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197264–65-70">[70]</a></sup> In return, Socrates warned jurors and Athenians that criticism of them by his many disciples was inescapable, unless they became good men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMay200047–48-59">[59]</a></sup> After a delay caused by Athenian religious ceremonies, Socrates spent his last day in prison. His friends visited him and offered him an opportunity to escape, which he declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197220_&amp;_65–66Ober2010146_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197220_&amp;_65–66Ober2010146-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The question of what motivated Athenians to convict Socrates remains controversial among scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013302_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013302-72">[72]</a></sup> There are two theories. The first is that Socrates was convicted on religious grounds; the second, that he was accused and convicted for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013302_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013302-72">[72]</a></sup> Another, more recent, interpretation synthesizes the religious and political theories, arguing that religion and state were not separate in ancient Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013323_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013323-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The argument for religious persecution is supported by the fact that Plato's and Xenophon's accounts of the trial mostly focus on the charges of impiety. In those accounts, Socrates is portrayed as making no effort to dispute the fact that he did not believe in the Athenian gods. Against this argument stands the fact that many skeptics and atheist philosophers during this time were not prosecuted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013319–322_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013319–322-74">[74]</a></sup> According to the argument for political persecution, Socrates was targeted because he was perceived as a threat to democracy. It was true that Socrates did not stand for democracy during the reign of the Thirty Tyrants and that most of his pupils were against the democrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013307–308_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013307–308-75">[75]</a></sup> The case for it being a political persecution is usually challenged by the existence of an amnesty that was granted to Athenian citizens in 403 BC to prevent escalation to civil war after the fall of the Thirty. However, as the text from Socrates's trial and other texts reveal, the accusers could have fuelled their rhetoric using events prior to 403 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013303–304_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERalkowski2013303–304-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_method">Socratic method</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">Socratic method</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="787" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg/220px-The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg/330px-The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg/440px-The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Debate_Of_Socrates_And_Aspasia_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Debate of Socrates and Aspasia</i> by <a href="/wiki/Nicolas-Andr%C3%A9_Monsiau" title="Nicolas-André Monsiau">Nicolas-André Monsiau</a>. Socrates's discussions were not limited to a small elite group; he engaged in dialogues with foreigners and with people from all social classes and of all genders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201153_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201153-77">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A fundamental characteristic of Plato's Socrates is the Socratic method, or the method of refutation (<i>elenchus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011179Wolfsdorf201334–35_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011179Wolfsdorf201334–35-78">[78]</a></sup> It is most prominent in the early works of Plato, such as <i>Apology</i>, <i>Crito</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)#Book_I" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic I</a></i>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfsdorf201334_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfsdorf201334-79">[79]</a></sup> The typical <i>elenchus</i> proceeds as follows. Socrates initiates a discussion about a topic with a known expert on the subject, usually in the company of some young men and boys, and by dialogue proves the expert's beliefs and arguments to be contradictory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011182–184Wolfsdorf201334–35_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011182–184Wolfsdorf201334–35-80">[80]</a></sup> Socrates initiates the dialogue by asking his interlocutor for a definition of the subject. As he asks more questions, the interlocutor's answers eventually contradict the first definition. The conclusion is that the expert did not really know the definition in the first place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011184_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011184-81">[81]</a></sup> The interlocutor may come up with a different definition. That new definition, in turn, comes under the scrutiny of <a href="/wiki/Socratic_questioning" title="Socratic questioning">Socratic questioning</a>. With each round of question and answer, Socrates and his interlocutor hope to approach the truth. More often, they continue to reveal their ignorance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972125–127_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972125–127-82">[82]</a></sup> Since the interlocutors' definitions most commonly represent the mainstream opinion on a matter, the discussion places doubt on the common opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972128–129_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972128–129-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates also tests his own opinions through the Socratic method. Thus Socrates does not teach a fixed philosophical doctrine. Rather, he acknowledges his own ignorance while searching for truth with his pupils and interlocutors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972128–129_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972128–129-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have questioned the validity and the exact nature of the Socratic method, or indeed if there even <i>was</i> a Socratic method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011179,185-193_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011179,185-193-84">[84]</a></sup> In 1982, the scholar of ancient philosophy <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Vlastos" title="Gregory Vlastos">Gregory Vlastos</a> claimed that the Socratic method could not be used to establish the truth or falsehood of a proposition. Rather, Vlastos argued, it was a way to show that an interlocutor's beliefs were inconsistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185Wolfsdorf201334–35Ambury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185Wolfsdorf201334–35Ambury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter-85">[85]</a></sup> There have been two main lines of thought regarding this view, depending on whether it is accepted that Socrates is seeking to prove a claim wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185Wolfsdorf201344Ambury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185Wolfsdorf201344Ambury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter-86">[86]</a></sup> According to the first line of thought, known as the constructivist approach, Socrates indeed seeks to refute a claim by this method, and the method helps in reaching affirmative statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2011185-87">[87]</a></sup> The non-constructivist approach holds that Socrates merely wants to establish the inconsistency between the premises and conclusion of the initial argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmbury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmbury2020The_Elenchus:_Socrates_the_Refuter-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_priority_of_definition">Socratic priority of definition</span></h3>
<p>Socrates starts his discussions by prioritizing the search for definitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2013136_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2013136-89">[89]</a></sup> In most cases, Socrates initiates his discourse with an expert on a subject by seeking a definition—by asking, for example, what virtue, goodness, justice, or courage is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2013136–139Ahbel-Rappe201171_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2013136–139Ahbel-Rappe201171-90">[90]</a></sup> To establish a definition, Socrates first gathers clear examples of a virtue and then seeks to establish what they had in common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972112_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972112-91">[91]</a></sup> According to Guthrie, Socrates lived in an era when sophists had challenged the meaning of various virtues, questioning their substance; Socrates's quest for a definition was an attempt to clear the atmosphere from their radical skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972110–111_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972110–111-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars have argued that Socrates does not endorse the priority of definition as a principle, because they have identified cases where he does not do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2013143–145Bett2011228_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2013143–145Bett2011228-93">[93]</a></sup> Some have argued that this priority of definition comes from Plato rather than Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2013143–145,_147Bett2011229_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2013143–145,_147Bett2011229-94">[94]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Peter_Geach" title="Peter Geach">Peter Geach</a>, accepting that Socrates endorses the priority of definition, finds the technique fallacious. Αccording to Geach, one may know a proposition even if one cannot define the terms in which the proposition is stated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenson2013145_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenson2013145-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_ignorance">Socratic ignorance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg/220px-Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg/330px-Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg/440px-Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delfi_Apollons_tempel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Apollo_(Delphi)" title="Temple of Apollo (Delphi)">Temple of Apollo</a> at Delphi, where Pythia was sited. The Delphic aphorism <i><a href="/wiki/Know_thyself" title="Know thyself">Know thyself</a></i> was important to Socrates, as evident in many Socratic dialogues by Plato, especially <i>Apology</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011144_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011144-96">[96]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Plato's Socrates often claims that he is aware of his own lack of knowledge, especially when discussing ethical concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Arete" title="Arete">arete</a> (i.e., goodness, courage) since he does not know the nature of such concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972122Bett2011215McPartland201394–95_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972122Bett2011215McPartland201394–95-97">[97]</a></sup> For example, during his trial, with his life at stake, Socrates says: "I thought Evenus a happy man, if he really possesses this art (<i>technē</i>), and teaches for so moderate a fee. Certainly I would pride and preen myself if I knew (<i>epistamai</i>) these things, but I do not know (<i>epistamai</i>) them, gentlemen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland201398_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland201398-98">[98]</a></sup> In some of Plato's dialogues, Socrates appears to credit himself with some knowledge, and can even seem strongly opinionated for a man who professes his own ignorance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013108–109_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013108–109-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are varying explanations of the Socratic inconsistency (other than that Socrates is simply being inconsistent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013117_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013117-100">[100]</a></sup> One explanation is that Socrates is being either ironic or modest for pedagogical purposes: he aims to let his interlocutor to think for himself rather than guide him to a prefixed answer to his philosophical questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013119_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013119-101">[101]</a></sup> Another explanation is that Socrates holds different interpretations of the meaning of "knowledge". Knowledge, for him, might mean systematic understanding of an ethical subject, on which Socrates firmly rejects any kind of mastery; or might refer to lower-level cognition, which Socrates may accept that he possesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013117–119_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013117–119-102">[102]</a></sup> In any case, there is consensus that Socrates accepts that acknowledging one's lack of knowledge is the first step towards wisdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013118–119_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013118–119-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates is known for disavowing knowledge, a claim encapsulated in the saying "<a href="/wiki/I_know_that_I_know_nothing" title="I know that I know nothing">I know that I know nothing</a>". This is often attributed to Socrates on the basis of a statement in Plato's <i>Apology</i>, though the same view is repeatedly found elsewhere in Plato's early writings on Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVlastos19851_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVlastos19851-104">[104]</a></sup> In other statements, though, he implies or even claims that he does have knowledge. For example, in Plato's <i>Apology</i> Socrates says: "...but that to do injustice and disobey my superior, god or man, this I <i>know</i> to be evil and base..." (<i>Apology</i>, 29b6–7).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVlastos19856–7_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVlastos19856–7-105">[105]</a></sup> In his debate with Callicles, he says: "...I <i>know</i> well that if you will agree with me on those things which my soul believes, those things will be the very truth..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVlastos19856–7_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVlastos19856–7-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whether Socrates genuinely thought he lacked knowledge or merely feigned a belief in his own ignorance remains a matter of debate. A common interpretation is that he was indeed feigning modesty. According to Norman Gulley, Socrates did this to entice his interlocutors to speak with him. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Terence_Irwin" title="Terence Irwin">Terence Irwin</a> claims that Socrates's words should be taken literally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVlastos19851-2Lesher1987275_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVlastos19851-2Lesher1987275-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gregory Vlastos argues that there is enough evidence to refute both claims. On his view, for Socrates, there are two separate meanings of "knowledge": Knowledge-C and Knowledge-E (C stands for "certain", and E stands for <i>elenchus</i>, i.e. the Socratic method). Knowledge-C is something unquestionable whereas Knowledge-E is the knowledge derived from Socrates's <i>elenchus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesher1987276_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesher1987276-107">[107]</a></sup> Thus, Socrates speaks the truth when he says he knows-C something, and he is also truthful when saying he knows-E, for example that it is evil for someone to disobey his superiors, as he claims in <i>Apology</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesher1987276Vasiliou201328_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesher1987276Vasiliou201328-108">[108]</a></sup> Not all scholars have agreed with this semantic dualism. James H. Lesher has argued that Socrates claimed in various dialogues that one word is linked to one meaning (i.e. in <i>Hippias Major</i>, <i>Meno</i>, and <i>Laches</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesher1987278McPartland2013123_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesher1987278McPartland2013123-109">[109]</a></sup> Lesher suggests that although Socrates claimed that he had no knowledge about the nature of virtues, he thought that in some cases, people can know some ethical propositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013123–124_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPartland2013123–124-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_irony">Socratic irony</span></h3>
<p>There is a widespread assumption that Socrates was an ironist, mostly based on the depiction of Socrates by Plato and Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2011239_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2011239-111">[111]</a></sup> Socrates's irony is so subtle and slightly humorous that it often leaves the reader wondering if Socrates is making an intentional pun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201320_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201320-112">[112]</a></sup> Plato's <i>Euthyphro</i> is filled with Socratic irony. The story begins when Socrates is meeting with Euthyphro, a man who has accused his own father of murder. When Socrates first hears the details of the story, he comments, "It is not, I think, any random person who could do this [prosecute one's father] correctly, but surely one who is already far progressed in wisdom". When Euthyphro boasts about his understanding of divinity, Socrates responds that it is "most important that I become your student".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201324Lane2011239_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201324Lane2011239-113">[113]</a></sup> Socrates is commonly seen as ironic when using praise to flatter or when addressing his interlocutors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2011249–251_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2011249–251-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars are divided on why Socrates uses irony. According to an opinion advanced since the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, Socratic irony is a playful way to get the audience's attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2011241–242_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2011241–242-115">[115]</a></sup> Another line of thought holds that Socrates conceals his philosophical message with irony, making it accessible only to those who can separate the parts of his statements which are ironic from those which are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane2011243_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane2011243-116">[116]</a></sup> Gregory Vlastos has identified a more complex pattern of irony in Socrates. On Vlastos's view, Socrates's words have a double meaning, both ironic and not. One example is when he denies having knowledge. Vlastos suggests that Socrates is being ironic when he says he has no knowledge (where "knowledge" means a lower form of cognition); while, according to another sense of "knowledge", Socrates is serious when he says he has no knowledge of ethical matters. This opinion is not shared by many other scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201328–29_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasiliou201328–29-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_eudaimonism_and_intellectualism">Socratic eudaimonism and intellectualism</span></h3>
<p>For Socrates, the pursuit of <i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i> motivates all human action, directly or indirectly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185Vlastos1991203_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185Vlastos1991203-118">[118]</a></sup> Virtue and knowledge are linked, in Socrates's view, to <i>eudaimonia</i>, but how closely he considered them to be connected is still debated. Some argue that Socrates thought that virtue and <i>eudaimonia</i> are identical. According to another view, virtue serves as a means to <i>eudaimonia</i> (the "identical" and "sufficiency" theses, respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReshotko2013158_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReshotko2013158-119">[119]</a></sup> Another point of debate is whether, according to Socrates, people desire what is in fact good—or, rather, simply what they <i>perceive</i> as good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReshotko2013158_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReshotko2013158-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Moral_intellectualism" title="Moral intellectualism">Moral intellectualism</a> refers to the prominent role Socrates gave to knowledge. He believed that all virtue was based on knowledge (hence Socrates is characterized as a <i>virtue</i> intellectualist). He also believed that humans were guided by the cognitive power to comprehend what they desire, while diminishing the role of impulses (a view termed <i>motivational</i> intellectualism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185-120">[120]</a></sup> In Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a></i> (345c4–e6), Socrates implies that "no one errs willingly", which has become the hallmark of Socratic virtue intellectualism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegvic2006171–173_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegvic2006171–173-121">[121]</a></sup> In Socratic moral philosophy, priority is given to the intellect as being the way to live a good life; Socrates deemphasizes irrational beliefs or passions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegvic2006171_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegvic2006171-122">[122]</a></sup> Plato's dialogues that support Socrates's <i>intellectual motivism</i>—as this thesis is named—are mainly the <i>Gorgias</i> (467c–8e, where Socrates discusses the actions of a tyrant that do not benefit him) and <i>Meno</i> (77d–8b, where Socrates explains to Meno his view that no one wants bad things, unless they do not know what is good and bad in the first place).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185–186_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013185–186-123">[123]</a></sup> Scholars have been puzzled by Socrates's view that <i><a href="/wiki/Akrasia" title="Akrasia">akrasia</a></i> (acting because of one's irrational passions, contrary to one's knowledge or beliefs) is impossible. Most believe that Socrates left no space for irrational desires, although some claim that Socrates acknowledged the existence of irrational motivations, but denied they play a primary role in decision-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013190–191_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrickhouseSmith2013190–191-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4067" data-file-width="2688" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg/220px-Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg/330px-Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg/440px-Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Euthyphro_Stephanus_1578_p_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henri_Estienne" title="Henri Estienne">Henri Estienne</a>'s 1578 edition of <i>Euthyphro</i>, parallel Latin and Greek text. Estienne's translations were heavily used and reprinted for more than two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAusland2019686–687_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAusland2019686–687-125">[125]</a></sup> Socrates's discussion with Euthyphro still remains influential in theological debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011117_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011117-126">[126]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Socrates's religious nonconformity challenged the views of his times and his critique reshaped religious discourse for the coming centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013257_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013257-127">[127]</a></sup> In Ancient Greece, organized religion was fragmented, celebrated in a number of festivals for specific gods, such as the <a href="/wiki/Dionysia" title="Dionysia">City Dionysia</a>, or in domestic rituals, and there were no sacred texts. Religion intermingled with the daily life of citizens, who performed their personal religious duties mainly with sacrifices to various gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013259–260_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013259–260-128">[128]</a></sup> Whether Socrates was a practicing man of religion or a 'provocateur atheist' has been a point of debate since ancient times; his trial included impiety accusations, and the controversy hasn't yet ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013257–258_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013257–258-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates discusses divinity and the soul mostly in <i><a href="/wiki/First_Alcibiades" title="First Alcibiades">Alcibiades</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Euthyphro" title="Euthyphro">Euthyphro</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)">Apology</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972151–153_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972151–153-130">[130]</a></sup> In <i>Alcibiades</i> Socrates links the human soul to divinity, concluding "Then this part of her resembles God, and whoever looks at this, and comes to know all that is divine, will gain thereby the best knowledge of himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972153_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972153-131">[131]</a></sup> His discussions on religion always fall under the lens of his rationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013260–262McPherran2011111_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013260–262McPherran2011111-132">[132]</a></sup> Socrates, in <i>Euthyphro</i>, reaches a conclusion which takes him far from the age's usual practice: he considers sacrifices to the gods to be useless, especially when they are driven by the hope of receiving a reward in return. Instead he calls for philosophy and the pursuit of knowledge to be the principal way of worshipping the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013265_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013265-133">[133]</a></sup> His rejection of traditional forms of piety, connecting them to self-interest, implied that Athenians should seek religious experience by self-examination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013266_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013266-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates argued that the gods were inherently wise and just, a perception far from traditional religion at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013263–266_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013263–266-135">[135]</a></sup> In <i>Euthyphro</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro_dilemma" title="Euthyphro dilemma">Euthyphro dilemma</a> arises. Socrates questions his interlocutor about the relationship between piety and the will of a powerful god: Is something good because it is the will of this god, or is it the will of this god because it is good?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013263McPherran2011117_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013263McPherran2011117-136">[136]</a></sup> In other words, does piety follow the good, or the god? The trajectory of Socratic thought contrasts with traditional Greek theology, which took <i><a href="/wiki/Eye_for_an_eye" title="Eye for an eye">lex talionis</a></i> (the eye for an eye principle) for granted. Socrates thought that goodness is independent from gods, and gods must themselves be pious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011117–119_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011117–119-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates affirms a belief in gods in Plato's <i>Apology</i>, where he says to the jurors that he acknowledges gods more than his accusers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013272–273_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013272–273-138">[138]</a></sup> For Plato's Socrates, the existence of gods is taken for granted; in none of his dialogues does he probe whether gods exist or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271-139">[139]</a></sup> In <i>Apology</i>, a case for Socrates being agnostic can be made, based on his discussion of the great unknown after death,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972157–158_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972157–158-140">[140]</a></sup> and in <i>Phaedo</i> (the dialogue with his students in his last day) Socrates gives expression to a clear belief in the immortality of the soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972160–164_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972160–164-141">[141]</a></sup> He also believed in oracles, divinations and other messages from gods. These signs did not offer him any positive belief on moral issues; rather, they were predictions of unfavorable future events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011123–127_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011123–127-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Xenophon's <i>Memorabilia</i>, Socrates constructs an argument close to the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Teleological_argument" title="Teleological argument">teleological intelligent-design argument</a>. He claims that since there are many features in the universe that exhibit "signs of forethought" (e.g., eyelids), a divine creator must have created the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271-139">[139]</a></sup> He then deduces that the creator should be omniscient and omnipotent and also that it created the universe for the advance of humankind, since humans naturally have many abilities that other animals do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271Long200963_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013270–271Long200963-143">[143]</a></sup> At times, Socrates speaks of a single deity, while at other times he refers to plural "gods". This has been interpreted to mean that he either believed that a supreme deity commanded other gods, or that various gods were parts, or manifestations, of this single deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013272Long200963_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2013272Long200963-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been a source of puzzlement how Socrates's religious beliefs can be consistent with his strict adherence to rationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011114–115_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011114–115-145">[145]</a></sup> Philosophy professor Mark McPherran suggests that Socrates interpreted every divine sign through secular rationality for confirmation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011124_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPherran2011124-146">[146]</a></sup> Professor of ancient philosophy <a href="/wiki/A._A._Long" title="A. A. Long">A. A. Long</a> suggests that it is anachronistic to suppose that Socrates believed the religious and rational realms were separate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong200964_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong200964-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_daimonion">Socratic daimonion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates,_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1480" data-file-width="1950" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg/220px-Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg/330px-Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg/440px-Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alcibades_being_taught_by_Socrates,_Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Alcibiades Receiving Instruction from Socrates</i>, a 1776 painting by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Vincent" title="François-André Vincent">François-André Vincent</a>, depicting Socrates's daimon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapatin2009146_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapatin2009146-148">[148]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In several texts (e.g., Plato's <i>Euthyphro</i> 3b5; <i>Apology</i> 31c–d; Xenophon's <i>Memorabilia</i> 1.1.2) Socrates claims he hears a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daemon_(classical_mythology)#Socrates" title="Daemon (classical mythology)">daimōnic sign</a>—an inner voice heard usually when he was about to make a mistake. Socrates gave a brief description of this daimonion at his trial (<i>Apology</i> 31c–d): "...The reason for this is something you have heard me frequently mention in different places—namely, the fact that I experience something divine and daimonic, as Meletus has inscribed in his indictment, by way of mockery. It started in my childhood, the occurrence of a particular voice. Whenever it occurs, it always deters me from the course of action I was intending to engage in, but it never gives me positive advice. It is this that has opposed my practicing politics, and I think its doing so has been absolutely fine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong200963–64_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong200963–64-149">[149]</a></sup> Modern scholarship has variously interpreted this Socratic <i>daimōnion</i> as a rational source of knowledge, an impulse, a dream or even a paranormal experience felt by an ascetic Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong200965–66,_70_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong200965–66,_70-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtue_and_knowledge">Virtue and knowledge</span></h3>
<p>Socrates's theory of virtue states that all virtues are essentially one, since they are a form of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131-151">[151]</a></sup> For Socrates, the reason a person is not good is because they lack knowledge. Since knowledge is united, virtues are united as well. Another famous dictum— "no one errs willingly"—also derives from this theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe2006164–165_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe2006164–165-152">[152]</a></sup> In <i>Protagoras</i>, Socrates argues for the unity of virtues using the example of courage: if someone knows what the relevant danger is, they can undertake a risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131-151">[151]</a></sup> Aristotle comments: " ... Socrates the elder thought that the end of life was knowledge of virtue, and he used to seek for the definition of justice, courage, and each of the parts of virtue, and this was a reasonable approach, since he thought that all virtues were sciences, and that as soon as one knew [for example] justice, he would be just..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131Ahbel-RappeKamtekar200972_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972131Ahbel-RappeKamtekar200972-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Love">Love</span></h3>
