<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_and_Alcibiades,_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1025" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg/220px-Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg/330px-Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg/440px-Socrates_and_Alcibiades%2C_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_and_Alcibiades,_Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Socrates and Alcibiades, by <a href="/wiki/Christoffer_Wilhelm_Eckersberg" title="Christoffer Wilhelm Eckersberg">Christoffer Wilhelm Eckersberg</a>, 1813–1816</div></div></div>
<p>Some texts suggest that Socrates had love affairs with <a href="/wiki/Alcibiades" title="Alcibiades">Alcibiades</a> and other young persons; others suggest that Socrates's friendship with young boys sought only to improve them and were not sexual. In <i>Gorgias</i>, Socrates claims he was a dual lover of Alcibiades and philosophy, and his flirtatiousness is evident in <i>Protagoras</i>, <i>Meno</i> (76a–c) and <i>Phaedrus</i> (227c–d). However, the exact nature of his relationship with Alcibiades is not clear; Socrates was known for his self-restraint, while Alcibiades admits in the <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i> that he had tried to seduce Socrates but failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013210–211_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013210–211-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Socratic theory of love is mostly deduced from <i><a href="/wiki/Lysis_(dialogue)" title="Lysis (dialogue)">Lysis</a></i>, where Socrates discusses love<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013211–212Rudebusch2009187_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013211–212Rudebusch2009187-155">[155]</a></sup> at a wrestling school in the company of Lysis and his friends. They start their dialogue by investigating parental love and how it manifests with respect to the freedom and boundaries that parents set for their children. Socrates concludes that if Lysis is utterly useless, nobody will love him—not even his parents. While most scholars believe this text was intended to be humorous, it has also been suggested that <i>Lysis</i> shows Socrates held an egoistic view of love, according to which we only love people who are useful to us in some way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013214–215_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013214–215-156">[156]</a></sup> Other scholars disagree with this view, arguing that Socrates's doctrine leaves room for non-egoistic love for a spouse; still others deny that Socrates suggests any egoistic motivation at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013212_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013212-157">[157]</a></sup> In <i>Symposium</i>, Socrates argues that children offer the false impression of immortality to their parents, and this misconception yields a form of unity among them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013231_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013231-158">[158]</a></sup> Scholars also note that for Socrates, love is rational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013230_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEObdrzalek2013230-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is notable that Socrates, who claims to know only that he does not know, makes an exception (in Plato's <i>Symposium</i>), where he says he will tell the truth about Love, which he learned from a 'clever woman'. Classicist <a href="/wiki/Armand_D%27Angour" title="Armand D'Angour">Armand D'Angour</a> has made the case that Socrates was in his youth close to <a href="/wiki/Aspasia" title="Aspasia">Aspasia</a>, and that Diotima, to whom Socrates attributes his understanding of love in <i>Symposium</i>, is based on her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED'Angour2019_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED'Angour2019-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socratic_philosophy_of_politics">Socratic philosophy of politics</span></h3>
<p>While Socrates was involved in public political and cultural debates, it is hard to define his exact political philosophy. In Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a>,</i> he tells <a href="/wiki/Callicles" title="Callicles">Callicles</a>: "I believe that I'm one of a few Athenians—so as not to say I'm the only one, but the only one among our contemporaries—to take up the true political craft and practice the true politics. This is because the speeches I make on each occasion do not aim at gratification but at what's best."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011333–341Johnson2013234_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011333–341Johnson2013234-161">[161]</a></sup> His claim illustrates his aversion for the established democratic assemblies and procedures such as voting—since Socrates saw politicians and rhetoricians as using tricks to mislead the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013234_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013234-162">[162]</a></sup> He never ran for office or suggested any legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011334_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011334-163">[163]</a></sup> Rather, he aimed to help the city flourish by "improving" its citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013234_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013234-162">[162]</a></sup> As a citizen, he abided by the law. He obeyed the rules and carried out his military duty by fighting wars abroad. His dialogues, however, make little mention of contemporary political decisions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Expedition" title="Sicilian Expedition">Sicilian Expedition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011334_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriswold2011334-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Socrates spent his time conversing with citizens, among them powerful members of Athenian society, scrutinizing their beliefs and bringing the contradictions of their ideas to light. Socrates believed he was doing them a favor since, for him, politics was about shaping the moral landscape of the city through philosophy rather than electoral procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013235_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013235-164">[164]</a></sup> There is a debate over where Socrates stood in the polarized Athenian political climate, which was divided between oligarchs and democrats. While there is no clear textual evidence, one widely held theory holds that Socrates leaned towards democracy: he disobeyed the one order that the oligarchic government of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Tyrants" title="Thirty Tyrants">Thirty Tyrants</a> gave him; he respected the laws and political system of Athens (which were formulated by democrats); and, according to this argument, his affinity for the ideals of democratic Athens was a reason why he did not want to escape prison and the death penalty. On the other hand, there is some evidence that Socrates leaned towards oligarchy: most of his friends supported oligarchy, he was contemptuous of the opinion of the many and was critical of the democratic process, and <i>Protagoras</i> shows some anti-democratic elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013236–237_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013236–237-165">[165]</a></sup> A less mainstream argument suggests that Socrates favoured democratic <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>, a theory that prioritizes active participation in public life and concern for the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013238_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013238-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yet another suggestion is that Socrates endorsed views in line with <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, a political ideology formed in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>. This argument is mostly based on <i>Crito</i> and <i>Apology</i>, where Socrates talks about the mutually beneficial relationship between the city and its citizens. According to Socrates, citizens are morally autonomous and free to leave the city if they wish—but, by staying within the city, they also accept the laws and the city's authority over them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013239–241_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013239–241-167">[167]</a></sup> On the other hand, Socrates has been seen as the first proponent of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a>. Socrates's strong objection to injustice, along with his refusal to serve the Thirty Tyrants' order to arrest Leon, are suggestive of this line. As he says in <i>Critias</i>, "One ought never act unjustly, even to repay a wrong that has been done to oneself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013241–242_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013241–242-168">[168]</a></sup> Ιn the broader picture, Socrates's advice would be for citizens to follow the orders of the state, unless, after much reflection, they deem them to be unjust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013255–256_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2013255–256-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_era">Hellenistic era</span></h3>

class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_Efes_Museum.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2257" class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">Carnelian</a> gem imprint representing Socrates, Rome, 1st century BC–1st century AD (left); Wall painting at a house depicting Socrates, 1st–5th century AD, Museum of <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Socrates's impact was immense in philosophy after his death. With the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonists</a>, almost all philosophical currents after Socrates traced their roots to him: Plato's Academy, Aristotle's Lyceum, the Cynics, and the Stoics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197299_&amp;_165Long2011355Ahbel-Rappe201195–96_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie197299_&amp;_165Long2011355Ahbel-Rappe201195–96-170">[170]</a></sup> Interest in Socrates kept increasing until the third century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011355–356_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011355–356-171">[171]</a></sup> The various schools differed in response to fundamental questions such as the purpose of life or the nature of <i><a href="/wiki/Arete" title="Arete">arete</a></i> (virtue), since Socrates had not handed them an answer, and therefore, philosophical schools subsequently diverged greatly in their interpretation of his thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972165–166Long2011355–357_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972165–166Long2011355–357-172">[172]</a></sup> He was considered to have shifted the focus of philosophy from a study of the natural world, as was the case for pre-Socratic philosophers, to a study of human affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011358_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011358-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immediate followers of Socrates were his pupils, <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid of Megara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristippus" title="Aristippus">Aristippus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antisthenes" title="Antisthenes">Antisthenes</a>, who drew differing conclusions among themselves and followed independent trajectories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972169_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972169-174">[174]</a></sup> The full doctrines of Socrates's pupils are difficult to reconstruct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972179–183_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972179–183-175">[175]</a></sup> Antisthenes had a profound contempt of material goods. According to him, virtue was all that mattered. Diogenes and the Cynics continued this line of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972170_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972170-176">[176]</a></sup> On the opposite end, Aristippus endorsed the accumulation of wealth and lived a luxurious life. After leaving Athens and returning to his home city of <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Cyrene</a>, he founded the <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaics" title="Cyrenaics">Cyrenaic philosophical school</a> which was based on <a href="/wiki/Hedonism#Cyrenaic_school" title="Hedonism">hedonism</a>, and endorsing living an easy life with physical pleasures. His school passed to his grandson, bearing the same name. There is a dialogue in Xenophon's work in which Aristippus claims he wants to live without wishing to rule or be ruled by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972170–174_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972170–174-177">[177]</a></sup> In addition, Aristippus maintained a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">skeptical</a> stance on <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, claiming that we can be certain only of our own feelings. This view resonates with the Socratic understanding of ignorance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972175–177_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1972175–177-178">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclid</a> was a contemporary of Socrates. After Socrates's trial and death, he left Athens for the nearby town of <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a>, where he founded a school, named the Megarians. His theory was built on the pre-Socratic <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>. Euclid continued Socrates's thought, focusing on the nature of virtue.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoics</a> relied heavily on Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011362_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011362-179">[179]</a></sup> They applied the Socratic method as a tool to avoid inconsistencies. Their moral doctrines focused on how to live a smooth life through wisdom and virtue. The Stoics assigned virtue a crucial role in attaining happiness and also prioritized the relation between goodness and ethical excellence, all of which echoed Socratic thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011362–264_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011362–264-180">[180]</a></sup> At the same time, the philosophical current of <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a> claimed Socrates as its predecessor, in ethics and in its theory of knowledge. <a href="/wiki/Arcesilaus" title="Arcesilaus">Arcesilaus</a>, who became the head of the Academy about 80 years after its founding by Plato, radically changed the Academy's doctrine to what is now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_Skepticism" title="Academic Skepticism">Academic Skepticism</a>, centered on the Socratic philosophy of ignorance. The Academic Skeptics competed with the Stoics over who was Socrates's true heir with regard to ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011364–365_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011364–365-181">[181]</a></sup> While the Stoics insisted on knowledge-based ethics, Arcesilaus relied on Socratic ignorance. The Stoics' reply to Arcesilaus was that Socratic ignorance was part of Socratic irony (they themselves disapproved the use of irony), an argument that ultimately became the dominant narrative of Socrates in later antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011367_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011367-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Aristotle considered Socrates an important philosopher, Socrates was not a central figure in Aristotelian thought. One of Aristotle's pupils, <a href="/wiki/Aristoxenus" title="Aristoxenus">Aristoxenus</a> even authored a book detailing Socrates's scandals.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureans</a> were antagonistic to Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011368–369_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011368–369-183">[183]</a></sup> They attacked him for superstition, criticizing his belief in his <i>daimonion</i> and his regard for the oracle at Delphi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELong2011374_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELong2011374-184">[184]</a></sup> They also criticized Socrates for his character and various faults, and focusing mostly on his irony, which was deemed inappropriate for a philosopher and unseemly for a teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampos-Daroca2019240Lane2011244Long2011370_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampos-Daroca2019240Lane2011244Long2011370-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonists</a> were also antagonistic to Socrates, accusing him of being a prater about ethics, who engaged in mock humility, and who sneered at and mocked people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_world">Medieval world</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sughrat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="561" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/220px-Sughrat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/330px-Sughrat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/440px-Sughrat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sughrat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of Socrates in a manuscript by <a href="/wiki/Al-Mubashshir_ibn_Fatik" title="Al-Mubashshir ibn Fatik">Al-Mubashshir ibn Fatik</a></div></div></div>
<p>Socratic thought found its way to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_history#Early_Caliphate" title="Muslim history">Islamic Middle East</a> alongside that of Aristotle and the Stoics. Plato's works on Socrates, as well as other ancient Greek literature, were translated into Arabic by early Muslim scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Hayyan" title="Jabir ibn Hayyan">Jabir ibn Hayyan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mu%CA%BFtazila" title="Muʿtazila">Muʿtazila</a>. For Muslim scholars, Socrates was hailed and admired for combining his ethics with his lifestyle, perhaps because of the resemblance in this regard with <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s personality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlon2009317–318_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlon2009317–318-187">[187]</a></sup> Socratic doctrines were altered to match Islamic faith: according to Muslim scholars, Socrates made arguments for monotheism and for the temporality of this world and rewards in the next life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlon2009325–326_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlon2009325–326-188">[188]</a></sup> His influence in the Arabic-speaking world continues to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlon2009332_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlon2009332-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>In medieval times, little of Socrates's thought survived in the Christian world as a whole; however, works on Socrates from Christian scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a> were maintained in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, where Socrates was studied under a Christian lens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrizio2019609–610Hankins2009352_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrizio2019609–610Hankins2009352-190">[190]</a></sup> After the fall of Constantinople, many of the texts were brought back into the world of Roman Christianity, where they were translated into Latin. Overall, ancient Socratic philosophy, like the rest of classical literature before the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, was addressed with skepticism in the Christian world at first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHankins2009337–340_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHankins2009337–340-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, two different narratives of Socrates developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHankins2009348–349_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHankins2009348–349-192">[192]</a></sup> On the one hand, the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanist movement</a> revived interest in classical authors. <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> translated many of Plato's Socratic dialogues, while his pupil <a href="/wiki/Giannozzo_Manetti" title="Giannozzo Manetti">Giannozzo Manetti</a> authored a well-circulated book, a <i>Life of Socrates</i>. They both presented a civic version of Socrates, according to which Socrates was a humanist and a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>. Bruni and Manetti were interested in defending secularism as a non-sinful way of life; presenting a view of Socrates that was aligned with Christian morality assisted their cause. In doing so, they had to censor parts of his dialogues, especially those which appeared to promote homosexuality or any possibility of <a href="/wiki/Pederasty" title="Pederasty">pederasty</a> (with Alcibiades), or which suggested that the Socratic <i>daimon</i> was a god.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHankins2009341–346_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHankins2009341–346-193">[193]</a></sup> On the other hand, a different picture of Socrates was presented by Italian Neoplatonists, led by the philosopher and priest <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Marsilio Ficino</a>. Ficino was impressed by Socrates's un-hierarchical and informal way teaching, which he tried to replicate. Ficino portrayed a holy picture of Socrates, finding parallels with the life of Jesus Christ. For Ficino and his followers, Socratic ignorance signified his acknowledgement that all wisdom is God-given (through the Socratic <i>daimon</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHankins2009346–348_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHankins2009346–348-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael,_Socrate,_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade,_1675._Huile_sur_toile,_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg,_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3543" data-file-width="3270" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg/220px-Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg/330px-Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg/440px-Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael%2C_Socrate%2C_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade%2C_1675._Huile_sur_toile%2C_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg%2C_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reyer_Jacobsz._van_Blommendael,_Socrate,_ses_deux_%C3%A9pouses_et_Alcibiade,_1675._Huile_sur_toile,_210_x_198_cm._Strasbourg,_Mus%C3%A9e_des_Beaux-Art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Socrates along with his wives (he was married once or twice) and students, appears in many paintings. Here <i><a href="/wiki/Socrates,_his_two_Wives,_and_Alcibiades" title="Socrates, his two Wives, and Alcibiades">Socrates, his two Wives, and Alcibiades</a></i>, a painting by the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Reyer_van_Blommendael" title="Reyer van Blommendael">Reyer van Blommendael</a>. Often, his wife Xanthippe, alone or with <a href="/wiki/Myrto" title="Myrto">Myrto</a> (the other alleged wife of Socrates) is depicted emptying a pot of urine (hydria) over Socrates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapatin2009133–139_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapatin2009133–139-195">[195]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_France" title="Early modern France">early modern France</a>, Socrates's image was dominated by features of his private life rather than his philosophical thought, in various novels and satirical plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLean2009353–354_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLean2009353–354-196">[196]</a></sup> Some thinkers used Socrates to highlight and comment upon controversies of their own era, like <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_de_Viau" title="Théophile de Viau">Théophile de Viau</a> who portrayed a Christianized Socrates accused of atheism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLean2009355_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLean2009355-197">[197]</a></sup> while for <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, the figure of Socrates represented a reason-based theist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoughlin2019665_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoughlin2019665-198">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a> wrote extensively on Socrates, linking him to rationalism as a counterweight to contemporary religious fanatics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201112_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe201112-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/German_idealism" title="German idealism">German idealism</a> revived philosophical interest in Socrates, mainly through <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Hegel</a>'s work. For Hegel, Socrates marked a turning point in the history of humankind by the introduction of the principle of free subjectivity or self-determination. While Hegel hails Socrates for his contribution, he nonetheless justifies the Athenian court, for Socrates's insistence upon self-determination would be destructive of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sittlichkeit" title="Sittlichkeit">Sittlichkeit</a></i> (a Hegelian term signifying the way of life as shaped by the institutions and laws of the State).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2019751–753_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2019751–753-200">[200]</a></sup> Also, Hegel sees the Socratic use of rationalism as a continuation of Protagoras' focus on human reasoning (as encapsulated in the motto <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_mensura" title="Homo mensura">homo mensura</a></i>: "man is the measure of all things"), but modified: it is our reasoning that can help us reach objective conclusions about reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowman2019753,_761–763_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowman2019753,_761–763-201">[201]</a></sup> Also, Hegel considered Socrates as a predecessor of later ancient skeptic philosophers, even though he never clearly explained why.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2009373–374_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2009373–374-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a> considered Socrates his teacher,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019820_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019820-203">[203]</a></sup> and authored his master's thesis on him, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Irony_with_Continual_Reference_to_Socrates" title="The Concept of Irony with Continual Reference to Socrates">The Concept of Irony with Continual Reference to Socrates</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824-204">[204]</a></sup> There he argues that Socrates is not a moral philosopher but is purely an ironist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuench2009389_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuench2009389-205">[205]</a></sup> He also focused on Socrates's avoidance of writing: for Kierkegaard, this avoidance was a sign of humility, deriving from Socrates's acceptance of his ignorance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824–825_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824–825-206">[206]</a></sup> Not only did Socrates not write anything down, according to Kierkegaard, but his contemporaries misconstrued and misunderstood him as a philosopher, leaving us with an almost impossible task in comprehending Socratic thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchurYamato2019824-204">[204]</a></sup> Only Plato's <i>Apology</i> was close to the real Socrates, in Kierkegaard's view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuench2009390_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuench2009390-207">[207]</a></sup> In his writings, he revisited Socrates quite frequently; in his later work, Kierkegaard found ethical elements in Socratic thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuench2009389_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuench2009389-205">[205]</a></sup> Socrates was not only a subject of study for Kierkegaard, he was a model as well: Kierkegaard paralleled his task as a philosopher to Socrates. He writes, "The only analogy I have before me is Socrates; my task is a Socratic task, to audit the definition of what it is to be a Christian", with his aim being to bring society closer to the Christian ideal, since he believed that Christianity had become a formality, void of any Christian essence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuench2009390–391_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuench2009390–391-208">[208]</a></sup> Kierkegaard denied being a Christian, as Socrates denied possessing any knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMuench2009394_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMuench2009394-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> resented Socrates's contributions to Western culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837-210">[210]</a></sup> In his first book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_Tragedy" title="The Birth of Tragedy">The Birth of Tragedy</a></i> (1872), Nietzsche held Socrates responsible for what he saw as the deterioration of ancient Greek civilization during the 4th century BC and after. For Nietzsche, Socrates turned the scope of philosophy from pre-Socratic naturalism to rationalism and intellectualism. He writes: "I conceive of [the Presocratics] as precursors to a reformation of the Greeks: but not of Socrates"; "with Empedocles and Democritus the Greeks were well on their way towards taking the correct measure of human existence, its unreason, its suffering; they never reached this goal, thanks to Socrates".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2009408–409Ambury2020Legacy:_How_Have_Other_Philosophers_Understood_Socrates?_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2009408–409Ambury2020Legacy:_How_Have_Other_Philosophers_Understood_Socrates?-211">[211]</a></sup> The effect, Nietzsche proposed, was a perverse situation that had continued down to his day: our culture is a Socratic culture, he believed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837-210">[210]</a></sup> In a later publication, <i><a href="/wiki/Twilight_of_the_Idols" title="Twilight of the Idols">The Twilight of the Idols</a></i> (1887), Nietzsche continued his offensive against Socrates, focusing on the arbitrary linking of reason to virtue and happiness in Socratic thinking. He writes: "I try to understand from what partial and idiosyncratic states the Socratic problem is to be derived: his equation of reason = virtue = happiness. It was with this absurdity of a doctrine of identity that he fascinated: ancient philosophy never again freed itself [from this fascination]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2009410–411_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2009410–411-212">[212]</a></sup> From the late 19th century until the early 20th, the most common explanation of Nietzsche's hostility towards Socrates was his anti-rationalism; he considered Socrates the father of European <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>. In the mid-20th century, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Walter_Kaufmann_(philosopher)" title="Walter Kaufmann (philosopher)">Walter Kaufmann</a> published an article arguing that Nietzsche admired Socrates. Current mainstream opinion is that Nietzsche was ambivalent towards Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837–839_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaymond2019837–839-213">[213]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG/220px-Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG/330px-Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG/440px-Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_en_Biblioteca_Nacional.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue of Socrates outside the National Library of Uruguay, <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Continental_philosophy" title="Continental philosophy">Continental philosophers</a> <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, after experiencing the horrors of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, amidst the rise of totalitarian regimes, saw Socrates as an icon of individual conscience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011127_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011127-214">[214]</a></sup> Arendt, in <i><a href="/wiki/Eichmann_in_Jerusalem" title="Eichmann in Jerusalem">Eichmann in Jerusalem</a></i> (1963), suggests that Socrates's constant questioning and self-reflection could prevent the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banality_of_evil" title="Banality of evil">banality of evil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011137–138_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011137–138-215">[215]</a></sup> Strauss considers Socrates's political thought as paralleling Plato's. He sees an elitist Socrates in Plato's <i>Republic</i> as exemplifying why the <i>polis</i> is not, and could not be, an ideal way of organizing life, since philosophical truths cannot be digested by the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011138–140_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011138–140-216">[216]</a></sup> Popper takes the opposite view: he argues that Socrates opposes Plato's totalitarian ideas. For Popper, Socratic individualism, along with Athenian democracy, imply Popper's concept of the "open society" as described in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Open_Society_and_Its_Enemies" title="The Open Society and Its Enemies">Open Society and Its Enemies</a></i> (1945).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011140–142_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhbel-Rappe2011140–142-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
